    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="80e433edf43f4eb7b695c2240f83e464">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'aeec7a2d-2043-49ed-ae79-06d32dc5a2c4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d7157165" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Sodiophobic graphite can be converted to sodiophilic graphite intercalation compounds through solvated-sodium-ion co-intercalation mechanisms, thus facilitating dendrite-free sodium metal plating/stripping with enhanced Coulombic efficiencies. This finding propels the achievement of cost-effective and high capacity anodes for advanced sodium ion batteries with energy densities of above 250 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9d4dd557-a4c5-412a-ae3e-ea6ddf3ce829/anie202410253-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9d4dd557-a4c5-412a-ae3e-ea6ddf3ce829/anie202410253-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c1f0c24-d9c0-482c-a31d-6f6cfdb037a4/anie202410253-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9d4dd557-a4c5-412a-ae3e-ea6ddf3ce829/anie202410253-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d7157168" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Owing to the crustal abundance of sodium element, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a promising complementary to lithium-ion battery for stationary energy storage applications. The cointercalation chemistry enables the use of cost-effective graphite as anodes, whereas the low capacity (&lt;130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and high redox potential (&gt;0.6 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) of graphite significantly limit the energy density of SIBs. Herein, we induce the high-capacity Na metal into sodiophilic ternary graphite intercalation compounds (<i>t</i>-GICs) via co-intercalation and deposition reactions, thereby achieving Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes with high capacities and low working voltage (0.18 V). The new anodes exhibit high coulombic efficiencies of above 99.7 % over 550 cycles and a high-rate capacity of 588.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C (10 min per charge). When it is paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) cathodes, the SIBs demonstrate a high energy density of 259 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>both electrodes</sub> surpassing that of commercial LiFePO<sub>4</sub>//graphite batteries. The outstanding anode performance is attributed to the tailored sodiophilicity of graphite through manipulating the ether solvents and the in situ generated space among <i>t</i>-GIC flakes to stably accommodate Na metal. Our findings for stable Na plating/striping on sodiophilic graphite materials provide an effective approach for developing advanced SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410253-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410253-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Leveraging the merits of sodium's abundant and globally uniform distribution, SIBs are increasingly viewed as a superior alternative to current lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale energy storage applications. During the past few years, great efforts have been devoted to exploring suitable electrode materials for the commercialization of cost-effective SIBs. Among the potential anodes, Na metal with the highest capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and the lowest redox potential (−2.714 V of standard hydrogen electrode) has been considered an ideal choice.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0001_R_d7157155e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the drastic volume changes and dendritic growth that are typically accompanied in the stripping and plating of Na metal cause unsatisfactory coulombic efficiencies and safety issues.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0002_R_d7157155e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0003_R_d7157155e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span> Regarding the use of hard carbon anodes, their high cost and moderate capacities induce high energy-cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0004_R_d7157155e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0007_R_d7157155e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Natural graphite has been considered another promising anode for SIBs owing to its cost-effectiveness and accumulated know-how in commercial LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0008_R_d7157155e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">8</a></sup></span> Despite the initial infeasibility of Na ion insertion into graphite due to the thermodynamic instability of the binary Na-intercalated graphite compound (<i>b</i>-GIC), a Na<sup>+</sup>-ether co-intercalation chemistry was demonstrated to form <i>t</i>-GIC with remarkable reversibility and fast Na intercalation kinetics (i.e., 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C over 1000 cycles).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0009_R_d7157155e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0012_R_d7157155e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the high working potential (0.6–0.8 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and inferior capacity (less than 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) make graphite incomparable to the hard carbon and Na metal anode. By tailoring the Na co-intercalation potential in graphite through electrolyte engineering, we have uplifted the energy density of graphite-based Na ion full cells to 120 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0011_R_d7157155e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span> which still falls short in competing with commercial LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0013_R_d7157155e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span> The development of graphite anodes with high-capacity and low working voltage that can lead to SIBs with high energy and power densities remains challenging.</p>
         
         <p>The integration of high-capacity yet dendritic depositing Na metal with cost-effective graphite anodes appears promising. However, it is widely recognized that Li metal plated on graphite anodes are detrimental because of the deactivation of the plated Li or aggressive formation of Li dendrites on fully lithiated graphite, which could induce poor reversibility and the possibly short circuit of LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0014_R_d7157155e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Sci. Rev.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0015_R_d7157155e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Joule">15</a></sup></span> It was reported that the dendrite deposition of Li metal on graphite was mainly caused by the crowded Li ions at the edge of graphite under high charging rate or low temperature operation and the slow solid-state Li-ion diffusion in graphite galleries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0016_R_d7157155e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Today">16</a></sup></span> This insight from LIB electrochemistry is discouraging for the investigation of high-capacity graphite/alkali metal composite anodes. When we compare the Li-ion intercalation and solvated-Na-ion co-intercalation chemistries in graphite,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0017_R_d7157155e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span> it shows that the reaction kinetics of co-intercalation is drastically faster than the conventional Li ion intercalation process. It is inferred that dendritic Na formation may be absent on the surface of graphite due to the rapid co-intercalation reaction, which remains unexplored.</p>
         
         <p>A critical factor influencing Na metal deposition behavior is the sodiophilic property of substrate materials. A sodiophilic host can promote the Na nucleation for more uniform electrodeposition, resulting in improved efficiency and cyclic stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0018_R_d7157155e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span> For example, sodiophilic heteroatom-doped carbon materials<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0019_R_d7157155e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sci.">19</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0021_R_d7157155e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span> and Sn, Au, and Sb metals<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0022_R_d7157155e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0024_R_d7157155e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span> have been widely reported for Na metal anodes with extended cycle life at high current densities. These progresses are attained at expense of low-yield and high-cost. Moreover, some Na metal hosts contribute negligible capacities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0020_R_d7157155e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0021_R_d7157155e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0023_R_d7157155e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0025_R_d7157155e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Lett.">25</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0026_R_d7157155e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Lett.">26</a></sup></span> If the cost-effective graphite could be applied to accommodate substantial amounts of Na metal, the practical feasibility of SIB technology would be propelled a step forward. Unfortunately, pristine graphite was calculated to be strongly sodiophobic.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0027_R_d7157155e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Proc. Nat. Acad. Sci.">27</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we introduced a sodiophilic <i>t</i>-GIC by tailoring the co-intercalating solvents that can accommodate abundant Na metal through co-intercalation and plating reactions with high reversibility. The Na/<i>t</i>-GIC hybrid anodes exhibited high coulombic efficiencies (CEs) of above 99.7 % over 550 cycles and a high specific capacity of 588.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C (10 min per charge), which is more than five times of pure graphite anode. When the new anode is paired with NVPF cathodes, the NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cells present a high energy density of 254.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of anodes and cathodes (including active material, binder, super P, and solvent). Our comparative investigations confirm that graphite in ester or long-chain ether-based electrolyte cannot replicate the combined co-intercalation and plating reactions in such a reversible manner, indicating that the unique feature of <i>t</i>-GIC formed in dimethoxyethane (DME) electrolyte is different from pristine graphite or GICs for hosting Na metal. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and micro-computed tomography (CT) observations demonstrated uniform Na metal filling the voids among <i>t</i>-GIC flakes, thereby suppressing Na dendrite growth, and making good use of the in situ generated volume space among intercalated graphite flakes. Our findings suggest that the realization of high capacity and stable Na anodes through tailoring sodiophilicity of graphite can be effective, which strategy are ready to be extended to other metal anodes in conjunction with the low-cost graphite.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410253-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410253-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410253-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410253-sec-0003-title">2.1 Sodiophilicity of <i>t</i>-GICs Regulated by Co-Intercalation Reaction</h3>
            
            <p>The chemical structures of <i>t</i>-GICs can play significant roles in regulating the subsequent Na metal deposition behaviour for high-capacity Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1a</a>. It is necessary to screen the electrolyte formulas allowing both rapid Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation and reversible Na metal plating/striping in graphite electrodes. In a context of regular ester-based electrolytes (i.e., 1 mol L<sup>−1</sup> (M) NaPF<sub>6</sub> ethylene carbonate (EC)/propylene carbonate (PC), 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC)), the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into graphite is minimal and the Na plating/striping is unfavourable (Figure S1–S3). For linear ether-based electrolytes, our work has demonstrated excellent reversibility for Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation in graphite with capacities of around 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0009_R_d7157155e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0011_R_d7157155e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span> Ether-based electrolytes are also favourable for Na plating/striping processes (Figure S4 and S5), due to the formation of NaF-rich solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers on the surface of Na metal (Figure S6a), consistent with previous reports.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0028_R_d7157155e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0030_R_d7157155e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater.">30</a></sup></span> In addition, the beneficial SEI components are also detected on the surface of <i>t</i>-GIC (Figure S6 b and c), which may contribute to the stable Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes later.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410253-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4bd94890-f449-46b8-b039-4a7e729fe699/anie202410253-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4bd94890-f449-46b8-b039-4a7e729fe699/anie202410253-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8b67c885-0424-4cf1-ac58-0570c94eb52f/anie202410253-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4bd94890-f449-46b8-b039-4a7e729fe699/anie202410253-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410253-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance of the Na/<i>t</i>-GICs. (a) Formation of <i>t</i>-GIC (<span style="color:#acd7e5">light-blue region</span>) and Na plating/striping on Cu foil (light-orange region) illustrated by corresponding voltage profiles and schematics. (b) Voltage profiles of Na/<i>t</i>-GICs in three different electrolytes, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME, DEGDME, and DEGDME at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for Na-ion co-intercalation and 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for Na metal deposition during discharging, 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for Na stripping and de-intercalation during charging. Inset shows the schematic image of a fully discharging process and the enlarged view for Na nucleation overpotentials in these three electrolytes. (c) CEs of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME, Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DEGDME and <i>t</i>-GIC-TEGDME systems with the Na deposition capacities of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (top) and 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (bottom) at a current density of 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. (d) Selected voltage profiles of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME and Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DEGDME systems derived from (c). (e) Rate capacities and CEs of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME and Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DEGDME systems from 0.1 C to 6 C. 1.0 C means the current density for full discharging (or charging) in 1 hour. (f) Voltage profiles of Na/<i>t</i>-GICs in accordance with the results in (e).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Regarding the co-intercalation of solvent molecules and Na ions into graphite, the sodiophilicity of <i>t</i>-GICs can be adjusted by using ethers with different chain lengths. We prepared 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> solutions in a series of ethers, including DME, diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME), tetraethylene glycol dimethyl ether (TEGDME), poly (ethylene glycol) dimethyl ether PEG 250 (MW≈250), and PEG 500 (MW≈500). Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the co-intercalation-plating (discharge) and deintercalation-striping (charging) voltage profiles of graphite electrodes in these electrolytes. The detailed testing protocols are provided in the Experimental section. Graphite electrodes displayed high reversibility in DME- and DEGDME-based electrolytes with initial CEs of around 98.60 % and 98.90 % respectively with a Na metal deposition capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. However, in the TEGDME-based electrolyte, the reversibility of Na metal striping is poor with a CE of 40 %. This issue was exacerbated in PEG 250 and PEG 500-based electrolytes, which demonstrated irreversibility in Na metal stripping (Figure S7). These comparisons indicate that the reversibility of graphite or Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes is dependent on the chain length of the ether solvents. A closer examination of the Na plating curves in different ether electrolytes in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1b</a> shows that the seeding overpotential increased from 9.2 mV to 51.7 mV for DME- and TEGDME-based electrolytes. It means the ability of <i>t</i>-GICs to accommodate Na metal can also be influenced by the chain length of ether solvents.</p>
            
            <p>To evaluate the cyclic stability of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes, we conducted galvanostatic charge/discharge tests at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and fixed Na deposition capacities of 1 and 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> in DME-, DEGDME-, and TEGDME-based electrolytes. In Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1c</a>, the Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes show initial CEs of 89.51 %, 84.49 %, and 77.02 % in above three kinds of electrolytes, respectively. Note that the CE values were obtained by dividing the capacity of Na striping and Na<sup>+</sup>-ether deintercalation over that of Na<sup>+</sup>-ether co-intercalation and Na plating. The Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes can be stably cycled in DME-based electrolyte with high average CEs of 99.76 % over 550 cycles. In the DEGDME-based electrolyte, the Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrode can also be stably cycled for 350 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. In contrast, Na/<i>t</i>-GIC cycling in TEGDME-based electrolyte exhibited low CEs of around 50 %, consistent with its poor reversibility in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1b</a>. When we improved the Na deposition capacity to 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the initial CEs of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC cycling in DME- and DEGDME-based electrolytes are 91.51 % and 89.50 %, respectively, which were maintained at 99.78 % and 99.82 % in the following 300 cycles. The significantly overlapped voltage profiles in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1d</a> demonstrated the exceptional reversibility of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC in both DME- and DEGDME-based electrolytes.</p>
            
            <p>A significant advantage for co-intercalation reaction in graphite is the fast-charging capability. The high-rate performance of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes was examined in DME-based electrolyte in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1e</a>. When the current density was increased from 0.1 C to 6 C (1 C=0.65 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 1 C refers to the current density to fully discharge the electrode in 1 hour) by 60-folds, the capacity retention is almost 100 % (i.e., 588.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C <i>vs</i>. 588.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C). The exceptional high-rate capability indicated that the rapid co-intercalation reaction of graphite remains unaffected by rapid Na metal deposition and dissolution on the surface, even at high current rates. It is noteworthy that the specific capacities are calculated on base of the total mass of the deposited Na metal, co-intercalated solvent, and graphite. The specific capacities of around 580 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for Na/<i>t</i>-GIC are around five times that for pristine graphite electrodes. The average working voltage is 0.18 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, which is substantially lower than the 0.79 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for graphite cycling DME-based electrolyte (Figure S8). The lower redox potential and higher capacity for Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes would significantly benefit the energy densities of Na ion full cells. Notably, the impressive high-rate performance is also manifested in DEGDME-based electrolyte, verifying the effectiveness of sodiophilic <i>t</i>-GIC in achieving high-energy and power Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes. Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1f</a> shows the highly overlapped voltage profiles of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC cycling at increasing current densities from 0.1 C to 6 C in DME- and DEGDME-based electrolyte, in agreement with the high capacity retentions in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0001">1e</a>. Overall, Na/<i>t</i>-GICs presented appreciable electrochemical performance in both DME- and DEGDME-based electrolytes. Considering the relatively lower Na metal nucleation overpotential and the superior reversibility of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes in DME-based electrolyte, it was selected for further mechanistic studies.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410253-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410253-sec-0004-title">2.2 Na Plating/Striping Behaviours in <i>t</i>-GIC Electrodes</h3>
            
            <p>To elucidate the exceptional reversibility and high-rate capability of the Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes, we carried out morphological characterizations of the electrodes during a whole cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0002">2a</a>). Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0002">2b</a> presents the surface and cross-sectional scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the electrodes at representative discharging/charging stages. The pristine graphite electrode shows flat surface in the top view and a cross-sectional thickness of approximate 40 μm. Upon co-intercalating at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> to 0.01 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, a noticeable increase in the surface roughness is observed, whereas the electrode thickness increased significantly to ≈80 μm. The huge volume expansion is consistent with the interlayer distance change from 3.34 Å for graphite to 11.58 Å for <i>t</i>-GIC, to be discussed in the following X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis. Then, 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> of Na metal was deposited at a current density of 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> on co-intercalated graphite, which resulted in the appearance of Na metal on the surface and edges of <i>t</i>-GIC. Interestingly, average thickness of the electrode decreased by ≈8 % from 80 μm for <i>t</i>-GIC to 73 μm for Na/<i>t</i>-GIC. The self-shrinkage can be attributed to a space-filling deposition of Na metal as regulated by the sodiophilic <i>t</i>-GIC flakes. After Na deposition for another 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, all the internal space among graphite flakes was filled and the electrode thickness increased to ≈95 μm for the Na/<i>t</i>-GIC with 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> of Na metal. Further SEM observations show that almost all the basal planes of <i>t</i>-GIC flakes were covered by Na metal (Figure S9), suggesting a smooth deposition of Na metal on the surface of <i>t</i>-GIC. Importantly, the Na metal plated on the <i>t</i>-GIC is more uniform than that on Cu foil (Figure S10). The morphologies of <i>t</i>-GIC and graphite were recovered after Na striping and deintercalation reactions, indicative of the excellent reversibility of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes. A net thickness increase of 28 μm was observed after full desodiation, which may be caused by the rearrangement of graphite and the voids left by Na metal stripping.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0031_R_d7157155e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span> It is noted that several graphite electrodes were cycled and measured to ensure the reliability of above SEM observations.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410253-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/54a98cec-14c3-4811-bf0b-dfac06c47176/anie202410253-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/54a98cec-14c3-4811-bf0b-dfac06c47176/anie202410253-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6992c958-7d83-4cb2-9ae8-f32bfeb9dbe4/anie202410253-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/54a98cec-14c3-4811-bf0b-dfac06c47176/anie202410253-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410253-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Smooth and uniform spatial distribution of Na metal in the Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME. (a) A typical voltage profile of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC cycling in DME-based electrolyte with Na deposition current density of 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Vital states within one electrochemical cycle are highlighted. (b) SEM images exhibiting top and cross-sectional views of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME in the vital states. (c) Enlarged view and corresponding EDS mapping of the “2 h-Deposition” in (b). (d) Reconstructed CT image showing spatial distribution of Na in the “2 h-Deposition” Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The self-shrinkage of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes during Na plating warrants further investigation. Spatial distribution of Na metal (2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) in Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrode was visualized by micro-CT. Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0002">2d</a> shows a uniform distribution of Na metal among <i>t</i>-GICs flakes across a 100 μm×100 μm area, which contrasts with the large Na bumps (≈100 μm in size) on Cu foil in Figure S10. The energy -dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping of the cross-sectional Na/<i>t</i>-GIC also displayed dense Na deposit among <i>t</i>-GIC host (right of Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0002">2c</a>). These results suggest that Na metal preferentially deposits within the interstices among <i>t</i>-GIC flakes rather than on the upper surface of the electrode, which is a counterintuitive finding. This behavior indicates a pronounced sodiophilicity of <i>t</i>-GIC to promote the internal deposition of Na metal. The substantial interaction between <i>t</i>-GIC and Na likely contribute to an overall reduction in electrode thickness during the deposition process, a testament to the excellent cyclic stability of Na/t-GIC electrodes.</p>
            
            <p>In situ XRD measurements were further carried out to probe the phase evolution of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes during sodiation/desodiation. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0003">3b</a>, graphite undergoes a staging behaviour during the initial co-intercalation process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0013_R_d7157155e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0032_R_d7157155e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span> Typically, the main (002)-graphite peak (26.65°) referring to the 3.34 Å interlayer spacing between graphene layers is split into two new peaks at 24. 85° and 27.65° during sodiation. Both peaks are then shifted to lower or higher angles before remaining constant at 23.52° and 29.59°, referring to the formation of stage II <i>t</i>-GIC. As discharging further, three new peaks appeared at 15.1°, 22.8° and 30.5°, whose intensities increase by the consumption of the former two peaks, rendering another <i>t</i>-GIC phase of stage I, consistent with the previous work.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0033_R_d7157155e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> It is intriguing to observe a continuous peak shift to higher angles (Figure S11), corresponding to a lattice contraction in <i>c</i>-axis of <i>t</i>-GICs. The compacted <i>t</i>-GIC may be ascribed to the rearrangement of [Na-DME]<sup>+</sup> intercalants in graphite galleries. After terminating the co-intercalation reaction at 0.01 V, we applied a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for Na metal deposition. No noticeable XRD peak shift was observed for <i>t</i>-GICs after Na plating, suggesting marginal influence of Na metal on the crystal structure of <i>t</i>-GICs. In Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0003">3c</a>, no noticeable Na signal is exhibited possibly due to the poor visibility of the limited Na deposit amount in <i>t</i>-GIC (0.5 mAh cm<sup>2</sup>). When we increased the deposition capacity to 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Na metal peak at 29.6° can be clearly observed. These results are in accordance with the observed space-filling behaviour of Na metal among the <i>t</i>-GIC. Upon charging to 2 V, reversible striping and deintercalation reactions were demonstrated with the XRD peaks recovering to 26.65° for graphite.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410253-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82dae944-59c2-4ec7-9bf2-a231b7fc4520/anie202410253-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/82dae944-59c2-4ec7-9bf2-a231b7fc4520/anie202410253-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/32879db9-2532-4b26-9135-21cdfd289804/anie202410253-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82dae944-59c2-4ec7-9bf2-a231b7fc4520/anie202410253-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410253-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Reversible phase evolution of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC-DME and calculated sodiophilicity increase for <i>t</i>-GIC-DME. (a) In situ XRD spectra of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC in one reversible cycle. (b) Selected XRD spectra of representative stages. (c) Selected XRD spectra with 0.5 and 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na deposition. (d) Comparison of binding energy difference of Na to graphite and <i>t</i>-GIC-DME.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The distinctive sodiophilicity of <i>t</i>-GICs and graphite was investigated by density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The results in light-orange region of Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure S12 confirm the collective Na affinity improvement after the co-intercalation of [Na-DME]<sup>+</sup> in graphite. Specifically, the binding energies to Na ions for the (002) and (110) planes of graphite are increased by ~5 % and ~1 %, respectively. Taking the slit micropores (11.58 Å, Figure S12c) formed in the <i>t</i>-GIC into consideration, alternative attractive sites for Na metal are identified and illustrated in <span style="color:#90ed90">light-green</span> region in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0003">3d</a>. The significant binding energy difference of Na metal storage in interlayers of graphite and <i>t</i>-GIC further elucidates the sodiophilicity improvement. It also implies that the co-intercalation induced disadvantageous large interlayer expansion may turn into favourable accommodation sites for Na metal plating, in line with the above observed self-shrinkage phenomenon of <i>t</i>-GICs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410253-sec-0005">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410253-sec-0005-title">3 Full Cell Performance</h2>
         
         <p>To estimate the electrochemical performance of the high-capacity Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes in practical SIBs, we built NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cells in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> DME electrolyte. The NVPF//Cu-anode-less and NVPF//Graphite full cells were also assembled as control. NVPF is a representative cathode material in SIBs. Our solid-state synthesized NVPF presented a reversible capacity of ~115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and respectable cyclic reversibility for 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in NVPF//Na half cells (Figure S13). The electrochemical stability of the electrolyte used in full cell tests was examined in Figure S14, which indicated no significant oxidative currents up to 4.25 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0011_R_d7157155e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span> ensuring their reliable application in the Na ion full cells.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0004">4a</a> shows irreversible charging and discharging profiles for the NVPF//Cu anode-less full cells as initially discharging to 2.0 V, possibly due to the sacrifice of Na metal deposited on Cu foil. The NVPF//graphite cells presented an average discharging voltage of 3.05 V (Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0004">4b</a>) and energy density of 103.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, due to the relatively high working potential of graphite anodes. In contrast, NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC cells exhibited a high working voltage of 3.85 V (Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0004">4c</a>) and a high capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in a remarkably high energy density of 254.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of anodes and cathodes (including active material, binder, super P and intercalated solvent). Upon cycling, the cyclic capacities and average voltages of NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cells are maintained at around 107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 3.76 V (Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0004">4d</a>), respectively, rendering high energy densities of above 240 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> over 100 cycles with an exceptionally high energy retention of 95 %. The initial CEs of NVPF//Cu, NVPF//Graphite and NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC cells were calculated to be 42.5 %, 64.5 % and 93.3 %, respectively, demonstrating the outstanding reversibility for Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410253-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3c44a1fa-6269-4eaa-a0e8-174249d01380/anie202410253-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3c44a1fa-6269-4eaa-a0e8-174249d01380/anie202410253-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c0084fe0-a923-4d9a-8916-27720f809515/anie202410253-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3c44a1fa-6269-4eaa-a0e8-174249d01380/anie202410253-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410253-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Full cell performance. (a) Voltage profile of anode-free full cell NVPF//Cu measured with a voltage window of 2.0–4.25 V at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (b) Voltage profile of NVPF//G measured with a voltage window of 0.7–3.8 V at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, excluding capacity contribution by Na plating/stripping. (c) Voltage profiles of NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC measured with a voltage window of 2.0–4.25 V at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Comparison between the NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC and NVPF//G full cells with respect to specific capacity, CE, output voltage, and energy density. (e) Rate performance of NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC. (f) Ragone plots comparing the NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC and other reported full cells.<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0009" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0009_R_d7157155e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0034" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0034_R_d7157155e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0038" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0038_R_d7157155e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater. Technol.">38</a></sup></span> The dotted lines are indicators of time required for discharge. Current densities adopted for all full cell measurement are based on the weight of NVPF. Schematics of NVPF//Cu, NVPF//G, and NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cells are included in (a), (b), and (c), respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0004">4e</a> presents rate performance of the NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cells at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8 and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. More than 80 % of the capacity could be retained when the current density increased by 10-folds from 0.1 to 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC maintained energy densities of above 153 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> even under a fast charging current of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (around 6 min per charge). To estimate the standing of our NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cell, we summarized the energy and power densities of recently reported Na ion full cells in Figure <a href="#anie202410253-fig-0004">4f</a>. For a fair comparison, all the energy and power densities were normalized to their corresponding total mass of both anodes and cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0009_R_d7157155e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#anie202410253-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0034_R_d7157155e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#anie202410253-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410253-bib-0038_R_d7157155e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater. Technol.">38</a></sup></span> It clearly illustrates that the NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC full cells in this work surpass all the representative peers with regards to the energy densities, which can be attributed to the high capacity and low working potential of Na/<i>t</i>-GIC anodes. Considering the primary applications of SIBs for stationary energy storage systems, the low-cost of raw materials and competitive energy density would be essential parameters, thus endowing the NVPF//Na/<i>t</i>-GIC chemistry in this work to be an appealing choice.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410253-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410253-sec-0006-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have demonstrated the switching of sodiophobic graphite into sodiophilic <i>t</i>-GIC for dendrite-free Na metal deposition thus enabling high-capacity and cost-effective anodes in high-energy SIBs. Smooth and large amounts of Na metal deposition were achieved by utilizing the sodiophilic <i>t</i>-GIC substrate and the large empty spaces among <i>t</i>-GIC flakes, which are triggered by solvated-Na-ion co-intercalation reaction in DME-based electrolyte. The experimental and theoretical studies reveal that <i>t</i>-GIC with thermodynamically favourable Na nucleation sites can benefit the uniform Na metal coverage on <i>t</i>-GIC flakes. The optimal Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes can exhibit high coulombic efficiencies of above 99.7 % over 550 cycles and a high capacity of 588.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C (10 min per charge). The Na ion full cells with Na/<i>t</i>-GIC electrodes presented an outstanding energy density of 259.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a high energy retention of 95 % after 100 cycles under deep cycling at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides new insights towards the sodiophilicity of graphite chemistry and promising solutions to approach high-energy SIBs using cost-effective graphite materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410253-sec-0007">
